    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a74978102e5b49f78bd51a9fdf490738">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0f05ba37-b9aa-4409-b585-68c3fad5b2e7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Aiiso Yufeng Li Department of Chemical and Nano Engineering, University of California, La Jolla, San Diego, CA, 92093 USA</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Xu/Dapeng">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wu/Junlin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Junlin Wu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d43204347" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium all-solid-state batteries (NaSSBs) with an alloy-type anode (e.g., Sn and Sb) offer superior capacity and energy density compared to hard carbon anode. However, the irreversible loss of Na<sup>+</sup> at the alloy anode during the initial cycle results in diminished capacity and stability, impairing full-cell performance. This study presents an easy-to-implement cathode presodiation strategy by employing a Na-rich material to address these challenges. Leveraging the high theoretical capacity and suitable voltage window, Na<sub>2</sub>S is chosen as the Na donor, which is activated by creating a mixed electron-ion conducting network, delivering a high capacity of 511.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. By adding a small amount (i.e., 3&nbsp;wt.%) of Na<sub>2</sub>S to the cathode composite, a NaCrO<sub>2</sub> || Sn full cell demonstrated capacity improvement from 90.8 to 118.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on cathode mass). The capacity-balanced full cell can thus cycle to more than 300 times with &gt;90% capacity retention. This work provides a practical solution to enhance the full-cell performance and advance the transformation from half-cell to full-cell applications of NaSSBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405678-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the widespread adoption of electronic devices and the increasing use of renewable energies, interest in energy storage devices like batteries has surged. Despite the market dominance, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) face limitations due to resource availability and sustainability concerns. As an alternative, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gained attention due to earth-abundant sodium (Na) and cost-effective transition metal-based cathodes. However, the flammability of their electrolytes remains a significant safety concern.</p>
         
         <p>Na all-solid-state batteries (NaSSBs) have emerged as a safer technology that uses nonflammable and nonvolatile inorganic solid electrolytes (SEs), significantly reducing the risk of thermal runaway.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0001_R_d43204282e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0002_R_d43204282e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Storage Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the practical application of NaSSBs faces significant obstacles due to the low capacity and energy density resulting from the electrode selection, low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), and poor cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0003_R_d43204282e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett.">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0004_R_d43204282e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The hard carbon (HC) anode widely used in SIBs exhibits limited capacity, typically ≈250–320 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and a low volumetric energy density attributable to its low tapped density, often less than 1&nbsp;g cm<sup>−3</sup>. In addition, SEs must be added to the anode composite to form an ionic conducting pathway, reducing its energy density and increasing costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0005_R_d43204282e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0007_R_d43204282e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing a high-capacity anode is essential for boosting the energy densities of NaSSBs.</p>
         
         <p>Na metal anode is appealing in NaSSBs due to its high capacity and lowest potential. However, it is incompatible with most SEs, leading to detrimental issues such as persistent degradation reactions, dendritic growth, and low critical current density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0008_R_d43204282e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0010_R_d43204282e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na-containing alloys, such as sodium-tin (Na-Sn) alloy (e.g., Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>), are confronted with stability issues when exposed to ambient air, which poses significant challenges during fabrication processes. In contrast, Na-free alloy anodes such as Sn offer a notably higher capacity (847 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0011_R_d43204282e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and a high volumetric density of 7.3&nbsp;g cm<sup>−3</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0012_R_d43204282e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Power Sources">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> making them attractive for high energy density full cells. Moreover, the Na-free alloy anode demonstrates environmental compatibility and stability under atmospheric conditions, mitigating challenges associated with anode fabrication. Additionally, alloy-type anodes can function without needing SEs and carbon additives due to their inherent electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0013_R_d43204282e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Joule">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0014_R_d43204282e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Joule">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This property not only enhances cell energy density but also reduces the production costs of NaSSBs, making them more economically viable and sustainable for large-scale manufacturing.</p>
         
         <p>Despite the advantages above, NaSSB full cells with alloy anodes often experience inevitable Na<sup>+</sup> loss after the first charging/discharging cycle due to Na<sup>+</sup> trapping in the anode and forming anode/electrolyte interphases. This results in low ICE and considerably reduced energy density and cycling stability due to an&nbsp;unbalanced negative/positive (N/P) ratio in full cells. In this regard, the presodiation (pre-Na) strategy is crucial to mitigate the initial capacity loss, which can be realized by either anode pre-Na or cathode pre-Na methods.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0015_R_d43204282e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0019_R_d43204282e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chin. J. Chem. Eng.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Anode pre-Na materials (e.g., Na metal,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0017_R_d43204282e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0020_R_d43204282e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - RSC Adv.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> biphenyl/tetrahydrofuran)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0021_R_d43204282e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Appl. Electrochem.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> often exhibit a low potential and high chemical reactivity, akin to typical Na-containing alloy anodes. This method introduces significant manufacturing complexities and raises safety concerns, making their large-scale production impractical. Conversely, cathode pre-Na methods involve the addition of Na-rich additives into the cathode composites, offering the advantages of precise regulation of pre-Na capacity and compatibility with the widely used anode/cathode systems. The effectiveness of this method hinges on carefully selecting a high-capacity pre-Na agent to maximize the overall cell energy density. When designing pre-Na agents, three fundamental requirements should be considered. First, the pre-Na agent should exhibit significantly higher capacities than cathode materials, which is crucial for enhancing capacity. Second, it should ensure controlled Na<sup>+</sup> transport behavior below the charge cutoff voltage and avoid further reduction in the discharge potential range of the cathode. Third, the addition and decomposition of the&nbsp;pre-Na agent should not have detrimental effects such as impeding Na<sup>+</sup> transportation or releasing gases, which are crucial for maintaining stable and efficient battery performance over multiple charging/discharging cycles.</p>
         
         <p>Research on sacrificial pre-Na additives has primarily focused on liquid-based systems, with relatively little exploration within NaSSBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0022_R_d43204282e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0026_R_d43204282e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, many of these pre-Na agents exhibit electronic and ionic insulating properties. While this characteristic might not significantly impact liquid battery systems due to the good wettability and high ionic conductivity of liquid organic electrolytes, it is detrimental in NaSSBs. For example, one promising pre-Na candidate is Na<sub>2</sub>S, which meets all the design requirements. However, its ionic insulating nature (≈10<sup>−11</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) could impede Na<sup>+</sup> transport, leading to poor redox kinetics and low Na donation capacity in all-solid-state battery architectures. This situation underscores the need to activate Na<sub>2</sub>S as a pre-Na agent for NaSSBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we introduce a cathode pre-Na strategy by activating Na<sub>2</sub>S within a composite of Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> (NPS) and acetylene black (AB) (Na<sub>2</sub>S-NPS-C), in which NPS serves as the ionic conductor. At the same time, AB provides the necessary electronic conductivity. The composite's electronic conductivity reaches ≈10<sup>−3</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the ionic conductivity improves from the order of ≈10<sup>−11</sup> (for Na<sub>2</sub>S) to ≈10<sup>−5</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> (for the composite). An optimal NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S mass ratio (1:2) yields the highest first cycle charge capacity of 511.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to 90% of its theoretical capacity. When implemented in a NaCrO<sub>2</sub> (NCO) || Sn full cell configuration, the capacity (based on cathode mass) can increase from 90.8 to 118.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> by adding only 3&nbsp;wt.% of the pre-Na agent. After 1st cycle activation, no gas was released, and only ≈1.7&nbsp;wt.% of the inactive material remained in the cathode. Moreover, enhanced cycling performance was confirmed due to the continuous extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from pre-Na agent residue in the following cycles. As a result, the NCO||Sn full cell with cathode pre-Na agent shows significantly enhanced cycling performance with capacity retention of 90.4% after 300 cycles, far superior to 80.5% for cells without the pre-Na agent. The efficacy of this pre-Na technique in augmenting battery performance was also demonstrated in other cathode/anode combinations, including Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) cathode and Sb anode, showcasing its potential for broad applications across various NaSSB chemistries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405678-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405678-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Na Loss in the Alloy-Type Anode</h3>
            
            <p>To comprehensively evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport and kinetic characteristics in the Sn anode, a galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) test was conducted at 0.05 C, involving a charging/discharging period of 0.5 h followed by a 2-h rest interval. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0001">1b</a> illustrates the voltage profile during the first cycle of the Sn anode. The profile reveals a series of alloying processes of the Sn anode during sodiation (discharging step for Sn || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell), marked by the sequential formation of Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub>, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> phases, which are characterized by distinct voltage plateaus. For the subsequent desodiation (charging step for Sn || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell) process, the Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> alloy transforms back to Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub> with significant hysteresis. However, the rest of Na (≈0.8&nbsp;mol of Na per Sn) remains trapped within the anode due to the slow Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of the Na-Sn alloy at low Na content, which was also revealed by the evolution of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0001">1c</a>. Initially, the diffusion coefficient of the pristine Sn anode is ≈2×10<sup>−6</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. As Na begins to alloy with Sn at a potential of ≈0.40&nbsp;V (vs Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, the same below), the Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub> phase is formed, and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient drops sharply to ≈10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. This drastic decrease reflects the slow kinetic properties of the Na-Sn alloy at low Na content. As sodiation progresses, the formation of the Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> phase at the second plateau at 0.08&nbsp;V is accompanied by an increase in the diffusion coefficient (&gt;10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), indicating enhanced transport properties compared to the Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub> phase. At the end of the sodiation step, the Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> phase was formed with the potential decreases to −0.13&nbsp;V. A moderate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of ≈10<sup>−7</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> was obtained at this stage. For the desodiation (Na<sup>+</sup> extraction) process, a similar Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, ranging from high to low Na content, was observed at each stage of the Na–Sn alloy. As Na extraction nears the completion of the Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub> phase, the diffusion coefficient rapidly declines to ≈10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, indicating difficulty in removing the remaining Na. Consequently, some Na becomes irreversibly trapped within the anode, leading to permanent Na loss. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0001">1d</a> shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of the Sn anodes after charging/discharging in the half-cell to identify the composition of the products. The characteristic peaks of Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> were detected from the fully sodiated electrodes. After desodiation, the XRD pattern showed a strong signal of the Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub> phase and lower Na<sup>+</sup> content of NaSn<sub>2</sub>. Joshua et&nbsp;al. calculated the formation enthalpy per atom versus Na concentration in the Na–Sn compound and constructed the convex hull to show the stable structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0027_R_d43204282e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results show that compared with pure Sn, Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>1</sub> has a lower formation energy, ≈−0.02&nbsp;eV per atom, which results in a barrier for Na to return to the cathode during discharge. Such alloying components as discharged products were also observed in earlier works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0028_R_d43204282e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Rare Met.">28</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0029_R_d43204282e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405678-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e27948f5-1d4d-460f-b281-56bd794e962a/aenm202405678-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e27948f5-1d4d-460f-b281-56bd794e962a/aenm202405678-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/751260b4-7717-49a0-851d-6e7fe57e5dee/aenm202405678-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e27948f5-1d4d-460f-b281-56bd794e962a/aenm202405678-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405678-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405678" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations and mechanism study of Na<sup>+</sup> loss in NaSSB full cell with Sn anode during the 1<sup>st</sup> cycle. a) A scheme of cathode presodiation strategy used to compensate for Na trapping in the Sn anode and avoid Na loss in the cathode. b) Charge/discharge GITT voltage curves of Sn||Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell to show phase transition of Sn alloy anode and Na<sup>+</sup> trapping in the first cycle. c) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in different phases of Na-Sn alloy determined by GITT. d) XRD patterns of Na-Sn alloy after the 1st cycle of sodiation and desodiation. e) The 1st cycle charge/discharge curve of the NCO||Sn full cell and the NCO||Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell to illustrate their differences in ICE and highlight Na<sup>+</sup> trapping in the full cell. f) SEM images and EDX mapping of the Sn anode after the initial sodiation and g) desodiation process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The NCO || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half cells and NCO || Sn full cells were tested to elucidate the Na loss phenomena, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0001">1e</a>. For the NCO || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell, a high capacity of 117.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a high ICE of 97.61% was achieved. In contrast, a lower capacity of 94.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and ICE of 80.6% were achieved for the NCO || Sn full cell. In the NCO || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell, the excess Na inventory in the Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> counter electrode facilitates the restoration of Na stoichiometry in the NCO cathode, leading to a high ICE. Conversely, in the NCO || Sn full cell, a significant portion of Na<sup>+</sup> becomes trapped within the Sn anode during the alloying process, resulting in a much lower ICE.</p>
            
            <p>To study the morphology evolution and Na distribution, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0001">1f,g</a> show the focused ion beam milling-scanning electron microscopic (FIB-SEM) images and energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopic (EDX) mapping of the Sn anode in the full cell at the charged and discharged states, respectively. After charging (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0001">1f</a>), the Sn anode alloys with Na and experiences apparent volume expansion. The observed striped structure could be attributed to the different phases of Na-Sn alloy due to a&nbsp;nonuniform sodiation reaction. The EDX mapping of Na shown in the cyan areas suggests a higher Na concentration within the charged anode. After discharging, the particles contract when Na<sup>+</sup> is removed from the alloyed anode, with the thickness reduced from ≈16.1 to ≈6.8&nbsp;µm. While most of the Na is removed, a significant amount remains at the anode as shown in the EDX image, indicating irreversible Na trapping.</p>
            
            <p>The trapped Na results in a Na deficiency in the cathode, structural changes, and degradation in both the&nbsp;cathode and anode over subsequent cycles, leading to capacity decay in full cells. Introducing Na-rich additive with a high decomposition capacity to the cathode is logically feasible to compensate for the Na inventory loss due to these reasons. Hence, properly controlling the N/P ratio and the presodiation extent is critical to ensure enough Na in the cathode for stable battery cycling.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405678-sec-0040-title">2.2 Pre-Na Agent Design and Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The theoretical capacity is a primary consideration when selecting pre-Na agents. Composites with heavy anions and more complex structures may not be ideal due to their lower capacities, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Pre-Na agents with higher capacities (&gt;500 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) are advantageous because they enhance the battery energy density while minimizing the negative impact of introducing additional reaction products.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0030_R_d43204282e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0036_R_d43204282e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, simple compounds such as sodium nitride (Na<sub>3</sub>N), sodium oxide (Na<sub>2</sub>O), sodium phosphide (Na<sub>3</sub>P), and sodium peroxide (Na<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) exhibit potential (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2a</a>). However, for Na<sub>3</sub>N, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the nitrogen or oxygen gas generated in the decomposition process during the first charging cycle will cause volume expansion and the deterioration of the interface contact, thus increasing impedance and accelerating the cell performance degradation. Although Na<sub>3</sub>P boasts a high capacity of 804 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with no gas release, its low decomposition voltage (&lt;1&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) is incompatible with the electrochemical stability window of the widely used halide and sulfide SEs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0037_R_d43204282e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Alloy. Compd.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0038_R_d43204282e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Electrochim. Acta.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This incompatibility triggers reactions with SEs and the formation of insulating products, which significantly decrease the ionic conductivity, escalating the battery resistance and impedance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405678-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/af90d6dd-a449-4424-bff2-c9131606fea1/aenm202405678-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/af90d6dd-a449-4424-bff2-c9131606fea1/aenm202405678-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/82826d2c-d56e-47ec-9264-4541144b6dbf/aenm202405678-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/af90d6dd-a449-4424-bff2-c9131606fea1/aenm202405678-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405678-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405678" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Pre-Na agent design and characterizations. a) The theoretical capacity and the decomposition voltage of the potential cathode additives for presodiation. b) Scheme describing the preparation of the pre-Na agent using ball milling to mix Na<sub>2</sub>S, NPS, and AB. c) The XRD patterns of the pre-Na agents with various NPS and Na<sub>2</sub>S ratios (NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S) after ball milling. d) Diagram showing the calculated theoretical capacity and the trend of the actual capacity of pre-Na agent with different Na<sub>2</sub>S ratios. e) Ionic conductivity of different pre-Na agent compositions. f) The Arrhenius plot of the pre-Na agent was mixed by hand and ball milling to show the conductivity and activation energy. g) Nyquist plots of the hand-mixed and ball-milled pre-Na agent assembled with Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> as half-cell before charging. h) Scheme to show the pristine Na<sub>2</sub>S failing to conduct electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> and its activation by ball milled with NPS and AB to form ionic and electronic pathways.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In contrast, Na<sub>2</sub>S emerges as a more promising pre-Na agent with a moderate decomposition voltage (≈1.8&nbsp;V) and a high theoretical capacity of 687 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Unlike nitrides and oxides, its decomposition yields only elemental sulfur without gas production, avoiding volume expansion and gas-related degradation. Nevertheless, pure Na<sub>2</sub>S suffers from extremely low ionic conductivity (σ<sub>Na+</sub>) of 5.4 × 10<sup>−11</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) and is almost electronically insulating, which hinders its electrochemical oxidation and the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction during charging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0039_R_d43204282e938" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome these limitations, it is essential to create ionic and electronic transport pathways that facilitate the electrochemical decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S. Here, we introduced NPS to activate Na<sub>2</sub>S for pre-Na due to their similar chemical compositions. As a widely used SE in NaSSBs, NPS offers a notably high ionic conductivity (1 × 10<sup>−4</sup> to 2 × 10<sup>−4</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0040_R_d43204282e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Power Sources">40</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0041_R_d43204282e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with an oxidation voltage of ≈2.5 V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0042_R_d43204282e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Energy Lett.">42</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0044_R_d43204282e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is higher than the decomposition voltage of Na<sub>2</sub>S but lower than typical cathode operation voltage (e.g., NCO, NFM), making it an ideal choice for activating Na<sub>2</sub>S with minimal impact. The higher desodiation voltage of NPS allows Na<sub>2</sub>S to contribute Na<sup>+</sup> at a lower voltage, followed by Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from NPS itself, maximizing its capacity utilization. After the decomposition of NPS, there is a decrease in ionic conductivity for the pre-Na agent, which prevents Na<sup>+</sup> from returning and ensures the irreversible pre-Na process.</p>
            
            <p>Therefore, we designed Na<sub>2</sub>S-NPS-C pre-Na composites, which were synthesized by simple ball milling. The effects of ball milling and the varying ratios of Na<sub>2</sub>S and NPS in these composites were investigated. A scheme describing ball milling to obtain the pre-Na agent is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2b</a>. The large particles of Na<sub>2</sub>S were fragmented into smaller sizes during the ball milling process and combined with NPS particles and AB to obtain a higher ionic and electronic conductivity as a composite. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2c</a> shows the XRD patterns of Na<sub>2</sub>S, NPS, and synthesized pre-Na composites. After ball-milling, pre-Na agents exhibit identical peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S with increased peak intensity for higher Na<sub>2</sub>S content in pre-Na agents. The weak NPS peak indicates that the crystalline NPS was converted into an amorphous phase after the ball-milling process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0045_R_d43204282e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Powder Technol.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the Na<sub>2</sub>S peak was broadened, indicating the grain size reduction.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The initial charging capacity of pre-Na with different Na<sub>2</sub>S and NPS ratios is tested in a cell configuration of pre-Na agent || NPS || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2d</a>), and their charging voltage profiles are depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The theoretical capacity of the pre-Na agent was calculated based on the total Na<sup>+</sup> content in the composition (total mass of Na<sub>2</sub>S and NPS) (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#aenm202405678-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)):

               <div class="inline-equation" id="aenm202405678-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/363369e8-b74d-49a0-bead-65bd43f469e2/aenm202405678-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405678:aenm202405678-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/aenm202405678-math-0001.png">

                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>C<sub>t</sub></i> is the theoretical capacity of the&nbsp;pre-Na agent; <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f2451418-fb01-4cb3-8355-12a80df9f9ba/aenm202405678-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405678:aenm202405678-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202405678-math-0002.png">

                           <mi>S</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{\lambda }_{N{{a}_2}S}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> and λ<sub><i>NPS</i></sub> are the&nbsp;mass ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>S and NPS in pre-Na agent, respectively; <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/898badb4-2cc3-4567-bd9f-3963b6e2046b/aenm202405678-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202405678:aenm202405678-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202405678-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>C</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>N</mi>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>S</mi>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{C}_{N{{a}_2}S}}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math> and <i>C<sub>NPS</sub></i> are capacity for Na<sub>2</sub>S (687 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and NPS (352 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively.</div>
            
            <p>After ball milling with AB, pure NPS exhibits a capacity of 148.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after being charged to 4&nbsp;V, achieving only 42.3% of the theoretical value. This insufficient decomposition is likely due to the poor kinetics from the continuous decrease in <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> along with the process of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. The charging curve for pure NPS is a continuous sope, with no plateau at any specific voltage. As the Na<sub>2</sub>S content increases, the Na<sup>+</sup> donation capacity rises dramatically due to the&nbsp;substantial release of Na<sup>+</sup> from Na<sub>2</sub>S. The highest charge capacity of 511.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved at the mass ratio of NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S at 1:2, corresponding to an approximate molar ratio of 1:6. The theoretical capacity for this composition is estimated to be ≈575.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that near 90% of the Na<sup>+</sup> are utilized during 1st charging. However, a capacity decrease is observed when the Na<sub>2</sub>S ratio increases beyond this point. Only 417.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained at the ratio of NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S at 1:3. This reduction in capacity can be attributed to the decreased volume ratio of NPS electrolytes leading to poor percolation network for Na<sup>+</sup> conduction. The differential capacity (dQ/dV) curve (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) reveals the reaction plateaus of each composite ratio, with two characteristic peaks appearing at ≈2.0 and 2.3&nbsp;V, corresponding to the two stages of the phase transformation of the Na<sub>2</sub>S during the decomposition. At the end of the 1st stage at 2.1&nbsp;V, the 1:2 ratio sample achieves a capacity of 259.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, ≈60% of the Na<sub>2</sub>S theoretical capacity. This indicates an intermediate product of polysulfide Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> was generated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0046_R_d43204282e1177" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Small">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the 2nd stage, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> continuously loses Na, contributing to the remaining charge capacity. However, without mixing with NPS electrolyte, the peak intensity in the 2nd stage of the Na<sub>2</sub>S-AB mixture is inapparent (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Due to the low <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> of Na<sub>2</sub>S, it is difficult to fully activate Na<sub>2</sub>S by further extracting Na<sup>+</sup>, leading to a much lower capacity. Therefore, sufficient NPS is critical to maintain ion transport pathways within particles to fully extract the Na<sup>+</sup> capacity of the pre-Na agents.</p>
            
            <p>To reveal the electrochemical properties of the pre-Na composites with various ratios, their <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> and electronic conductivities (<i>σ</i><sub>e</sub>-) were measured and compared to quantitatively dissect the role of NPS and AB. The <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> of Na<sub>2</sub>S-NPS-C composites were measured by the DC polarization method using a symmetrical cell (Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> || NPS || Na<sub>2</sub>S-NPS-C || NPS || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>) as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a> (Supporting Information). The NPS layers function as electronic block layers, and their resistance was subtracted when calculating <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the pre-Na agent. The Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> alloy added on each side was a Na source and current collector. The <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the pre-Na agent with different NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S ratios were determined from the obtained value after applying a 0.1&nbsp;V bias voltage for 300 s, which shows a much-improved σ<sub>Na+</sub> than pure Na<sub>2</sub>S and an increasing trend with a higher NPS ratio as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a> (Supporting Information). Specifically, the <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> was 3.9 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, 1.8 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, and 1.2 × 10<sup>−5</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> for composites with NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S mass ratio of 1:1, 1:2, and 1:3, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2e</a>). The <i>σ</i><sub>e</sub>- of pure Na<sub>2</sub>S is extremely low, which behaves as an electronic insulator. However, after ball milling with AB, the <i>σ</i><sub>e-</sub> was improved to 1.3 × 10<sup>−2</sup>, 1.8 × 10<sup>−2</sup>, and 2.8 × 10<sup>−2</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> for the respective NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S ratios (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with the <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the pre-Na agents, their σ<sub>e</sub>- were nearly three orders of magnitude higher, which indicates that ionic conductivity is the main factor limiting charge transfer and capacity utilization. The NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S = 1:2 mixture with the highest specific Na<sup>+</sup> capacity was selected for further analysis.</p>
            
            <p>To understand the impact of the ball-milling process, the ionic conductivities of samples prepared by ball-milling and hand-mixing were measured at different temperatures to construct an Arrhenius curve. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2f</a>, the ball-milling samples exhibited higher ionic conductivity (about one magnitude) across all temperatures compared to the hand-mixing samples, with a lower activation energy (0.35 vs 0.44&nbsp;eV). Both samples were assembled into half-cells with Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> for electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2g</a> indicates that the hand-mixing sample exhibits no electrochemical activity. In contrast, the ball-milling sample showed an R<sub>p</sub> of ≈107 Ω, with the characteristic peak frequency of 10<sup>4</sup>&nbsp;Hz. This difference suggests that ball milling is essential to activate Na<sub>2</sub>S by forming a conductive pathway for ions and electrons between the particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0002">2h</a>).</p>
            
            <p>SEM and cryogenic transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) with EDX were utilized to characterize NPS and carbon distribution within the pre-Na agent composites. The pristine Na<sub>2</sub>S and NPS particles exhibit sizes ≈3.5 and 1.5&nbsp;µm, respectively (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0003">3a,b</a>). After the ball milling process, the large Na<sub>2</sub>S particles were reduced into smaller particles at ≈1.5&nbsp;µm, and the softer sulfide NPS particles were ground and dispersed around the Na<sub>2</sub>S particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0003">3c,d</a>). The SEM-EDX mapping of the&nbsp;pre-Na agent (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrates a uniform elemental distribution of S, P, Na, and C. Cryo-TEM shows lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.494&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (101) lattice of crystalline NPS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0003">3e</a>). Distances of 0.325 and 0.233&nbsp;nm are directed toward the (200) and (220) directions of Na<sub>2</sub>S, respectively, with these characteristic fringes filling most of the field of view as the primary phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0003">3f</a>). Cluster-like fringe structures can be observed, corresponding to typical carbon structures from the AB in the pre-Na agent. EDX mapping of a single particle of pre-Na composite reveals that NPS (characterized by the P signal) and Na<sub>2</sub>S are well-mixed in particle scale after ball milling. This shows the structural characteristics of the pre-Na agent and elucidates the mechanism of using NPS and AB to improve ion/electron conductivity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405678-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/202935ff-eb2e-42fb-91d1-355756cfc079/aenm202405678-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/202935ff-eb2e-42fb-91d1-355756cfc079/aenm202405678-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/12e90288-32cd-457b-80b6-82f810b5e360/aenm202405678-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/202935ff-eb2e-42fb-91d1-355756cfc079/aenm202405678-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
