            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405678-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0004">4a</a> presents the charge and discharge profile of the NCO cathode and the pre-Na agent in the voltage window of 2.25 to 3.5&nbsp;V. The NCO cathode exhibited a capacity of 117.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, while the pre-Na agent exhibited a significantly higher charging capacity of 511.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a negligible discharge capacity in the same voltage range. This property meets the requirement that after the pre-Na agent releases its stored Na<sup>+</sup> during the cathode charging process, it does not take in Na<sup>+</sup> during discharging. Further investigation was conducted on NCO cathode composites containing 60&nbsp;wt.% of active material and 1, 2, and 3 of pre-Na agent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0004">4b</a>). After aligning them to the same capacity, it was observed that Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from the pre-Na agent before reaching the NCO charging plateau. Adding 3&nbsp;wt.% of pre-Na agent to the composite increased the charging capacity from 119.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (w/o pre-Na agent) to 150.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. All cells displayed similar discharge capacities, indicating that the extra Na<sup>+</sup> transferred from the cathode is accumulated at the anode side.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405678-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d4fc2678-9528-4c09-9716-f27b800cacb9/aenm202405678-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d4fc2678-9528-4c09-9716-f27b800cacb9/aenm202405678-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f084a5e9-a24b-482a-bb9e-57286c5e78a9/aenm202405678-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d4fc2678-9528-4c09-9716-f27b800cacb9/aenm202405678-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405678-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405678" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterization of the NCO cathode composite with pre-Na agent added. a) Comparison of charging and discharging voltage curves between NCO cathode and pre-Na agent. b,c) Charge and discharge curves of the first cycle of NCO||Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell and NCO||Sn full-cell with different pre-Na agent ratios. d) Comparison of cathode discharge capacity and full cell energy density (based on the mass of cathode + anode + pre-Na) after adding the pre-Na agent. e) Long-term cycling performance of the NCO||Sn full cell with/without pre-Na agent.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We then tested NCO cathodes with and without the pre-Na agent in an NCO||Sn full cell with an N/P ratio of 1.1 and a voltage window of 1.75 to 3.6&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0004">4c</a>). All these cells show a similar ICE of ≈80% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), while their discharge capacity increases with more pre-Na being added in the cathode composite. With 3&nbsp;wt.% of pre-Na agent, the discharge capacity of the NCO||Sn full cell increased from 90.8 to ≈118.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, similar to the NCO||Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> half-cell, which corresponded to a 30.2% increase in capacity. This translates to a 21% increase in energy density based on the total weight of the cathode, anode, and pre-Na agent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0004">4d</a>). The full cell with the pre-Na additive also demonstrated stable cycling and significantly enhanced capacity retention (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0005">5e</a>). After 300 cycles at 0.5 C, the capacity retention of the full cell without pre-Na was 80.5%, while the cell with 3% pre-Na agent achieved a 90.4% capacity retention. This suggests that even though most of Na<sup>+</sup> has been extracted, the residual Na<sup>+</sup> in the pre-Na agent acts as a “Na<sup>+</sup> reservoir” that may continuously compensate for the Na<sup>+</sup> loss during the cycling, thereby enhancing cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0047_R_d43204282e1474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - SmartMat.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405678-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c04f069c-c278-42e5-a511-5799aaa0464a/aenm202405678-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c04f069c-c278-42e5-a511-5799aaa0464a/aenm202405678-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/26ac781d-a81f-4129-8280-9541b1dec311/aenm202405678-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c04f069c-c278-42e5-a511-5799aaa0464a/aenm202405678-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405678-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405678" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure and composition evolution of the cathode composite during the charging process. a,b) SEM images (backscattered mode) and EDX mapping of the cathode from the post-mortem cell showing a) the surface and the cross-section. c–e) XPS analysis of the C 1s, S 2p, and P 2p elements to characterize the change of chemical composition at different states of charge (SOC).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405678-sec-0060-title">2.4 The Pre-Na Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0005">5a,b</a> display the SEM images of the post-charging cathode, observed from the surface and cross-section, respectively. Under backscattering mode, dark spots were&nbsp;randomly dispersed within the cathode composite. EDX reveals that these areas contain sulfur (S) and phosphorus (P)-rich species, suggesting they are residues of pre-Na.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to determine the composition of these species. In the pristine pre-Na agent, the C─C bond seen in the C<sub>1s</sub> spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0005">5c</a>) indicates the presence of carbon (AB). The S 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0005">5d</a>) reveals distinctive PS<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> peaks relating to NPS at 161.6&nbsp;eV and Na<sub>2</sub>S at 159.6 eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0048_R_d43204282e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Mater.">48</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0049_R_d43204282e1526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The P 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0005">5e</a>) also displays a PS<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> peak at 131.4 eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0050_R_d43204282e1537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Mater. Chem. A">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the charging step, the decomposition of Na<sub>2</sub>S is proved by the reduction of its peak intensity and the appearance of new peaks at 163.8&nbsp;eV. This indicates the formation of elemental S as the final product.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0051_R_d43204282e1545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nano Energy">51</a>-<a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0053_R_d43204282e1545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nat. Commun.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Concurrently, the peak intensity of PS<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> decreases in both the S 2p and P 2p spectra, further confirming the decomposition of NPS. By the end of the charging process (100% SOC), the peaks for Na<sub>2</sub>S and PS<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> are no longer detectable. A significant increase in the S peak and a new peak at 162.4&nbsp;eV are observed, reflecting the formation of Na-S bonds and the polysulfide compound (Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>n</sub>) from Na<sub>2</sub>S decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0050_R_d43204282e1566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Mater. Chem. A">50</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0054_R_d43204282e1566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">54</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0055_R_d43204282e1566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, the P 2p spectrum shows new peaks at 133.2 and 134.2&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the oxidized product P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0056_R_d43204282e1575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - ACS Energy Lett.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>EIS and distribution of relaxing time (DRT) were used to analyze the decomposition process of the pre-Na agent at different SOCs. The cell was charged at 0.1 C and paused for an EIS test per hour (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). In the entire charging process, the cell Ohmic resistance (R<sub>Ω</sub>) was maintained at ≈480 Ω (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating no electrolyte-related degradation. As the SOC increases from 0% to 70% (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0006">6a,b</a>), the polarization resistance R2 (the&nbsp;equivalent circuit in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information) of the battery rose from 61.4 to 181.9 Ω, which relates to the charge-transfer reactions with the characteristic frequency at ≈10<sup>5</sup> to 10<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;Hz. This corresponds to the&nbsp;continuous extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from Na<sub>2</sub>S. When SOC is higher than 70%, a new semicircle with a characteristic frequency at 10<sup>3</sup> to 10<sup>1</sup> appears (R3/CPE3), indicating that NPS starts to decompose and leads to slower Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics inside pre-Na particles. As the Na<sup>+</sup> was further extracted from the pre-Na agent, R3 increased dramatically (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0006">6c</a>), resulting from the NPS decomposition and the depletion of Na<sup>+</sup> from pre-Na.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202405678-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eeab202c-7ef3-47e5-b615-ae4e05c72f88/aenm202405678-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eeab202c-7ef3-47e5-b615-ae4e05c72f88/aenm202405678-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6780ba07-a8eb-48c9-8f32-28a48bf57fe5/aenm202405678-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eeab202c-7ef3-47e5-b615-ae4e05c72f88/aenm202405678-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202405678-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202405678" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The evolution of the cathode composite impedance&nbsp;with the pre-Na agent during the 1<sup>st</sup> charging process. a–c) Nyquist plots of the cells with pre-Na agent as the cathode at SOC of 0%, 70%, and 100%, respectively. d–f) DRT results derived from the Nyquist plots with peaks fitted at different SOC (20%, 70%, and 100%) to show multiple electrochemical processes. g) A comparison of the DRT curves at SOC from 10% to 70% and h) 70% to 100%. i) All peak intensity evolution with SOC from 0% to 100%.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The DRT curves corresponding to different SOCs are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0006">6d–f</a>, which were deconvoluted to six different peaks (P1-P6), corresponding to the dominant electrochemical processes, further elucidating the decomposition process of the pre-Na agent. The P1 peak, with a time constant of 10<sup>−6</sup> s, remains unchanged during the charging process, corresponding to the resistance of Na<sup>+</sup> ions transferred across the SE grain boundaries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0057_R_d43204282e1654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Joule">57</a>, <a href="#aenm202405678-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202405678-bib-0058_R_d43204282e1654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - J. Power Sources">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Peak P2-P4, with time constants ranging from 10<sup>−5</sup> to 10<sup>−3</sup> s, are related to the charge transfer processes between the electrode and SE, including both the anode and cathode sides. These peaks gradually increase as the SOC increases from 0% to 70%, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0006">6g</a>. Given that abundant Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> alloy is used on the anode side, the increased impedance primarily contributes to the cathode side of the pre-Na agent. As Na<sup>+</sup> is gradually extracted from Na<sub>2</sub>S, the intermediate product of polysulfide Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> has lower electrochemical activity, leading to increased charge transfer resistance. When the pre-Na is further charged to SOC&gt; 70%, peaks P5 and P6 with a time constant from 10<sup>−3</sup> to 10<sup>−1</sup> s surge dramatically, with the peak shift to the higher time constant or lower characteristic frequency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0006">6g,i</a>). These processes are related to diffusion within the pre-Na agent. At this stage, final products of elemental S and P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> are generated, which impedes Na<sup>+</sup> transfer from the pre-Na. Concurrently, the decomposition of NPS begins to affect the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion of the pre-Na agent significantly. The DRT analysis supports the EIS discussion well and is consistent with our XPS results.</p>
            
            <p>To summarize, after the decomposition, the residue materials of polysulfides and elemental sulfur exhibit low conductivity and electrochemical activity. However, since only 1.7&nbsp;wt.% of residue remains in the cathode composite, it slightly reduces the conductivity and increases the impedance of the whole cathode composite. The small particle size scaling in several micrometers, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0005">5a,b</a>, will not affect the electrochemical properties of the bulk cathode significantly. The negative influence of the residue inactive materials is negligible when compared with the improvement from the pre-Na agent, especially reflected in the higher retention rate of the long cycle performance as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202405678-fig-0004">4e</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202405678-sec-0070-title">2.5 Broad Application of Pre-Na in NaSSBs</h3>
            
            <p>Due to the favorable decomposition voltage of Na<sub>2</sub>S, this pre-Na agent can be utilized in various NaSSB systems. To demonstrate its broad applicability, we tested it in full cells with NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) cathode and/or an antimony (Sb) anode. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a–c</a> (Supporting Information) showcases the initial charge and discharge cycles for three full-cell configurations: NFM || Sn, NFM || Sb, and NCO || Sb. The NFM cathode paired with the Sn anode achieved an ICE of 82%, similar to that with the NCO || Sn full cell. Introducing a 3% pre-Na agent to the NFM cathode enhanced the discharge capacity from 111.2 to 132.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Cells with Sb anode (NCO || Sb and NFM || Sb) exhibited lower ICEs (≈70%) due to more severe Na<sup>+</sup> trapping associated with Sb anode. Thus, they require a slightly higher amount (5 wt.%) of pre-Na agent to compensate for the increased Na<sup>+</sup> loss to recover the cathode capacity fully. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) provides a standardized calculation method for determining the pre-Na agent ratio. Additionally, this method is extended to accommodate various cathode (e.g., Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVP)) and anode (e.g., hard carbon) materials. Long-term cycling tests showed that these batteries with pre-Na agent maintained over 90% capacity retention after 300 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11d–f</a>, Supporting Information), highlighting the positive impact of pre-Na agent on battery performance as illustrated in NCO/Sn full cells.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405678-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>This study presents a universal cathode pre-Na strategy to address the first cycle irreversible capacity loss in NaSSBs. Na<sub>2</sub>S was used as the Na donor due to its high theoretical capacity, no gas generation, and suitable Na extraction voltage window. The activation of Na<sub>2</sub>S was achieved by creating a mixed electron-ion conducting network using NPS and AB. The ball milling process is essential for activating Na<sub>2</sub>S, as it significantly reduces the particle size of Na<sub>2</sub>S and increases contact with NPS and AB, ensuring a uniform percolation network. The pre-Na agent with the optimal ratio NPS: Na<sub>2</sub>S = 1:2 exhibits enhanced <i>σ</i><sub>Na+</sub> of 1.8 × 10<sup>−5</sup> and <i>σ</i><sub>e</sub>- of 1.8 × 10<sup>−2</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>, achieving a high capacity of 511 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. By incorporating a small amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S-NPS-C pre-Na agent into the cathode, the released Na<sup>+</sup> compensates for anode trapping, allowing the full cell to achieve the theoretical capacity, thereby maximizing energy density and advancing the development of NaSSBs. Additionally, cycling stability is improved due to the gradual release of residue Na<sup>+</sup> from the pre-Na agent, offering significant potential for next-generation high-energy-density batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202405678-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202405678-sec-0120-title"> Material Synthesis—Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> Electrolyte (NPS)</h4>
                  
                  <p>Two precursors, Na<sub>2</sub>S (Sigma–Aldrich 98%) and P<sub>2</sub>S<sub>5</sub> (Sigma–Aldrich 99%) were mixed with a pestle in the mortar. Then, the mixture was transferred into ball milling jars with a total mass of 1&nbsp;g, sealed with Y-ZrO<sub>2</sub> grinding media under an argon atmosphere. Ball milling was carried out by a ball miller machine (TMAX XQM) proceeding at 700 RPM for 6 h. Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> electrolyte powder was collected after ball milling without further treatment.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202405678-sec-0130-title"> Material Synthesis—Presodiation Agent</h4>
                  
                  <p>First, synthesized NPS, Na<sub>2</sub>S (Sigma–Aldrich 98%), and acetylene black (Thermo Scientific 99.9%) with the desired mass ratio were manually mixed in the mortar. The mixture was then transferred into ball milling jars and sealed with Y-ZrO<sub>2</sub> grinding media under an argon atmosphere. Ball milling was carried out by a ball miller machine (TMAX XQM) proceeding at 600 rpm for 5 h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202405678-sec-0140-title"> Material Synthesis—Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub></h4>
                  
                  <p>The Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> alloy was synthesized by ball-milling the sodium block and tin powder. First, the sodium block was manually mixed with the tin powder in the mortar with a pestle until pulverized, forming a powdery sodium–tin alloy. Then the mixture was transferred into ball milling jars and sealed with Y-ZrO<sub>2</sub> grinding media under an argon atmosphere, ball-milled at 500&nbsp;rpm for 2&nbsp;h to let the reaction sufficiently happened, and form a uniform sodium-tin alloy.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202405678-sec-0150-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>X-ray diffraction (XRD) samples were loaded into glass capillary tubes (Charles Supper) of 0.5&nbsp;mm diameter inside a glovebox, sealed with clay and wrapped with parafilm. Then, the samples were removed from the glovebox and flame-sealed using a butane torch. These samples were evaluated on a Bruker Kappa goniometer coupled with a Bruker APEX II Ultra diffractometer using Mo Kα (λ = 0.7093 Å) radiation at 40&nbsp;kV and 40&nbsp;mA within a Debye−Scherrer geometry.</p>
                  
                  <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) data was gathered using the AXIS Supra XPS from Kratos Analytical. The S 2p, P 2p, C 1s, and F 1s spectra were compiled and analyzed using CasaXPS software.</p>
                  
                  <p>Focused ion beam (FIB-SEM) was used to characterize the surface and cross-section morphology of the NPS films. The exposed and heat-treated films were mounted onto an SEM sample stage (Ted Pella) and transferred into the FEI Scios DualBeam FIB-SEM using the air-sensitive holder to avoid ambient air exposure. The exposed samples were prepared in a glovebox and sealed before being transferred to the focused ion beam scanning electron microscopy (FIB-SEM) instrument, effectively preventing contamination. EDS mapping was collected using a 10&nbsp;kV electron beam with a current of 0.1 nA.</p>
                  
                  <p>Raman spectroscopy was performed using a Renishaw inVia Raman Microscope and a 532&nbsp;nm laser source. Due to the samples' air sensitivity, all samples were sealed in two thin glass slides in the glove box and directly exposed to the laser beam during testing.</p>
                  
                  <p>TEM images were captured using Titan Cubed Themis Z 60–300, Thermo Fisher Scientific, with aberration correction, an X-FEG source, and a Bruker Super-X EDX detector. The test was operated at 300&nbsp;kV, with a 30 pA beam current, a 21.5 mrad convergence semi-angle, and an 80–379 mrad collection semi-angle. Atomistic models were generated using Crystalmaker software and Fast Fourier transform (FFT) patterns. The inverse analyses and radial intensity profiles were conducted using DigitalMicrograph (Gatan) software.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202405678-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202405678-sec-0160-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The ionic conductivity of the pre-Na agent was measured by the DC polarization method using a symmetrical cell (Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> || NPS || pre-Na agent || NPS || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>). All materials were added in a 10&nbsp;mm Polyetheretherketone (PEEK) die and pressed at 70&nbsp;MPa with titanium plungers. The NPS layers between the Na-Sn alloy, performing as the sodium source, and the pre-Na agent serve as the electron block layer to avoid short circuits from carbon. The σ<sub>Na+</sub> of the pre-Na agent with different Na<sub>2</sub>S-NPS ratios was determined from the obtained value after applying a 0.1&nbsp;V bias voltage for 300s, using Bio-Logic VSP-300.</p>
                  
                  <p>Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was measured with a frequency range from 7&nbsp;MHz to 1&nbsp;Hz and an AC amplitude of 10&nbsp;mV using Bio-Logic VSP-300. To test the EIS revolution of the pre-Na agent, the cell configuration as pre-Na || NPS || Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> was assembled and charged at 0.1C for 10h. To avoid the reaction between the NPS and Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> anode, which complicates the analysis and makes it challenging to identify the reaction on the cathode side, NBH was added between the SE layer and anode as an isolation layer. A 6&nbsp;mg pre-Na agent was weighed and placed on the NPS-NBH electrolyte layer as the cathode.</p>
                  
                  <p>DC polarization was collected by directly testing the pre-Na agent between the Ti plungers, while a potential bias of 1&nbsp;V was exerted to stimulate the electrochemical reaction.</p>
                  
                  <p>Solid-state batteries were assembled in a 10&nbsp;mm PEEK die with two Ti plungers. For pellet cell assembly, 100&nbsp;mg of commercial halide solid electrolyte (99.9%, Unigrid, USA) was pressed under 110&nbsp;MPa to form a rigid pellet without cracks as the support layer with a thickness of ≈400&nbsp;µm. To relieve the interfacial reaction at the SE-anode interface, 10&nbsp;mg NCBH (99.9%, Unigrid, USA) was added to form an isolated layer. For the presodiated cells, a 3% or 5% mass ratio of the pre-Na agent was mixed with NCO or NFM cathode composites. The NCO cathode composite was manually prepared by mixing NaCrO<sub>2</sub> active materials, halide SE, and vapor-grown carbon fibers (VGCF) in a 50:48:2 weight ratio and added to one side of the SE pellet. Since the NFM cathode has a lower conductivity than NCO, an extra ball milling step was required to help improve the cell performance. 0.4&nbsp;g tin or antimony powder was weighed and mixed with 0.2&nbsp;g CNT for anodes. Then, the mixture was distributed in 0.3&nbsp;g DI water in a THINKY Mixer at 1800&nbsp;rpm for 20&nbsp;min.</p>
                  
                  <p>The configuration of full cells was shown as NCO/NFM || halide SE || Sn/Sb. The assembled cells with all three parts were pressed again at 270&nbsp;MPa before electrochemical testing. The cells were cycled using a Neware Battery cycler at room temperature and under pressure at ≈75&nbsp;MPa.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
